📰

Current Affairs — IVC in Recent News

UNESCO 2021
Dholavira declared UNESCO World Heritage Site
Dholavira (Gujarat) was inscribed as a UNESCO World Heritage Site on 27 July 2021 — India's 40th WHS. It was recognized for its outstanding universal value as an IVC site with unique triple-division town planning and advanced water management. Excavated by R.S. Bisht since 1990.
DNA Study 2019
Rakhigarhi DNA Analysis — IVC Genetic Identity
Vasant Shinde (Deccan College) and Harvard's David Reich team published ancient DNA analysis from a 4,500-year-old Rakhigarhi skeleton. Key finding: The IVC person showed no Steppe-pastoralist ancestry (challenging Aryan invasion/migration timeline), descended from South Asian hunter-gatherers + Iranian-related farmers. The study suggests IVC developed indigenously.
Excavation 2023–24
New IVC Sites Discovered in Haryana and Rajasthan
ASI and state archaeology departments continue discovering Late Harappan and IVC-linked sites in Haryana's Ghaggar belt. The total count of known IVC/Harappan sites has now crossed 2,600, with India having more sites than Pakistan. Haryana has the highest concentration of IVC sites within India.
Climate Research 2022
Climate Change as Primary Driver — New Evidence
Studies using oxygen isotopes from Himalayan speleothems (cave formations) confirm a significant weakening of Indian Summer Monsoon between 2200–1900 BCE, coinciding precisely with the beginning of IVC decline. Cambridge (Petrie) and other teams are building a site-by-site archaeological-climate correlation model for IVC's de-urbanization.
📝

Previous Year Questions (UPSC)

Prelims PYQs

Prelims 2021 (Similar pattern)
With reference to the Indus Valley Civilization, which of the following statements is/are correct? (1) Cotton was cultivated in the IVC. (2) The IVC had trade relations with Mesopotamia. (3) Iron was extensively used by the IVC people.
Answer: 1 & 2 only. Cotton was first cultivated by IVC (Greeks called it sindon). Trade with Mesopotamia (Meluhha in Sumerian records) is well-documented via Lothal dockyard and Mesopotamian texts. Iron was ABSENT — IVC was a Bronze Age civilization. Iron appears in India only after c. 1000 BCE.
Prelims 2019
In which of the following sites of the Indus Valley Civilization, evidence of a tidal dockyard has been found? (a) Harappa (b) Mohenjo-daro (c) Lothal (d) Dholavira
Answer: (c) Lothal. S.R. Rao excavated Lothal (1954–63) and discovered a 222×37 m tidal dock with sluice gate mechanism — considered the world's first known tidal dockyard. Dholavira is known for water reservoirs, not a dockyard.
Prelims 2020 (Pattern)
The 'Pashupati Seal' found from which IVC site shows a cross-legged figure surrounded by animals?
Answer: Mohenjo-daro. The Pashupati Seal (steatite) was found at Mohenjo-daro. The figure is seated in yogic posture with a horned headdress, surrounded by elephant, rhinoceros, buffalo (right), tiger (left), and deer below. Marshall identified the figure as proto-Shiva/Pashupati.
Prelims 2018
Which IVC site is known for (1) evidence of the earliest ploughed field in the world, and (2) fire altars on the citadel?
Answer: Kalibangan (Rajasthan). The pre-Harappan level at Kalibangan shows the world's earliest ploughed field — two sets of furrows at right angles. The Mature Harappan citadel had a row of 7 fire altars. Excavated by A. Ghosh and B.B. Lal (1960–69). Also notable: only major IVC city without a Great Bath.
Prelims 2022 (Pattern)
With reference to Dholavira, recently in news, which of the following statements is correct? (a) It is the largest IVC site in India (b) It was inscribed as a UNESCO World Heritage Site in 2021 (c) It was excavated by B.B. Lal (d) It is located on the banks of the Indus river
Answer: (b) Dholavira was inscribed as UNESCO WHS on 27 July 2021 (India's 40th). It is NOT the largest IVC site in India — Rakhigarhi is. It was excavated by R.S. Bisht (not B.B. Lal). It is located on Khadir island in the Rann of Kutch (not on the Indus river).

Mains PYQs

Mains GS1 2018 — 15 marks
"The Indus Valley Civilization was far ahead of its time in urban planning." Critically examine this statement with reference to town planning, sanitation and public amenities.
Framework: (1) Introduction — IVC as Bronze Age urban civilization; 2550–1900 BCE peak; (2) Grid-pattern streets + standardized brick ratios 1:2:4; (3) Two-part city plan (Citadel + Lower Town); (4) Great Bath — ritual + public hygiene consciousness; (5) Drainage — underground brick drains, manholes, sump pits — unmatched in contemporaneous civilizations; (6) Wells (700+ at MJD); (7) Garbage bins on streets; (8) Critical note — our knowledge is limited by undeciphered script; (9) Conclusion — yes, far ahead especially in sanitation.
Mains GS1 2020 — 10 marks
Examine the different theories proposed for the decline of the Indus Valley Civilization. Which theory do you find most convincing and why?
Framework: (1) Brief each theory — Aryan Invasion (Wheeler; now discredited), Climate Change/Monsoon (Possehl, Giosan — PNAS 2012; currently most accepted), Drying of Saraswati/Ghaggar-Hakra (B.B. Lal), Tectonic Flooding (Raikes — specific to MJD), Trade Collapse, Multi-causal (Kenoyer); (2) Evaluation — Aryan Invasion largely rejected; Climate + River drying have strongest palaeoclimatic evidence; (3) Most convincing = multi-causal with climate change as primary driver — cite Rakhigarhi DNA evidence, eastward migration pattern, OCP culture as successor.

⚡ 10-Point Rapid Revision — Indus Valley Civilization

  1. Discovery: Harappa (1921, Daya Ram Sahni, Ravi river); Mohenjo-daro (1922, R.D. Banerjee, Indus); coined "IVC" — Sir John Marshall, 1924
  2. Extent: ~1.5 million sq km; W: Sutkagendor (Balochistan); E: Alamgirpur (UP); N: Manda (J&K); S: Daimabad (Maharashtra); 2,600+ sites
  3. Phases: Early Harappan 3200–2550 BCE (Mehrgarh, Kot Diji) → Mature 2550–1900 BCE (peak urban) → Late 1900–1200 BCE (de-urbanization; Cemetery H; OCP)
  4. Key Site Features: MJD = Great Bath + Granary + Priest-King bust + Bronze Dancing Girl; Lothal = Dockyard + fire altars; Kalibangan = earliest ploughed field + fire altars (NO Great Bath); Dholavira = 3-part plan + water reservoirs (UNESCO 2021); Rakhigarhi = largest site in India; Chanhudaro = only city with NO citadel
  5. Town Planning: Grid streets; 2-part city (Citadel + Lower Town); burnt brick 1:2:4; covered underground drains with manholes; Great Bath (11.88×7.01×2.43 m; bitumen waterproofed; ritual purpose)
  6. Economy: Bronze Age (no iron); cotton first cultivated; wheat+barley staples; trade with Mesopotamia (Meluhha); Lothal dockyard; weights — binary-decimal chert cubes; bead industry (carnelian etching)
  7. Seals: 4,000+ found; steatite; square with perforated boss; unicorn most common (~70%); Pashupati Seal (proto-Shiva; MJD) = yogic figure + 4 animals; used for trade identity
  8. Indus Script: ~400–600 signs; undeciphered; right-to-left; no bilingual text; Asko Parpola (proto-Dravidian hypothesis); I. Mahadevan (concordance)
  9. Religion/Art: Mother Goddess figurines (most common); Pashupati/proto-Shiva; phallus + ring stones; pipal tree; fire altars (Kalibangan, Lothal); NO identified temple or palace; Bronze Dancing Girl (lost-wax); Priest-King bust (steatite)
  10. Decline: Gradual 1900–1200 BCE; multi-causal — climate change/monsoon weakening (Possehl, Giosan, PNAS 2012 = most accepted), drying of Ghaggar-Hakra, tectonic flooding (MJD only), trade collapse; Aryan Invasion (Wheeler) — discredited; eastward migration → OCP culture
Continue with Vedic Civilization Topic 05: The Vedic Age — Rigvedic and Later Vedic periods, society, economy, religion
Topic 05

The Vedic Age (1500–600 BC)

Want personalized guidance on Ancient History strategy for UPSC? Our mentors have cleared the exam.

Book free 1-on-1 with mentors →

💡 Conceptual Clarity — 4 Key Distinctions for UPSC

  • Early Vedic ≠ Later Vedic: These are fundamentally different societies. Early Vedic (1500–1000 BCE) = pastoral, tribal, Punjab-based, Rigveda only, fluid Varna, high women's status. Later Vedic (1000–600 BCE) = agricultural, territorial states, Ganga plains, 3 new Vedas + Upanishads, rigid hereditary Varna, declined women's status
  • Vidatha disappeared, Sabha and Samiti survived: All three were Rigvedic assemblies but only Sabha and Samiti continued into the Later Vedic period. Vidatha (oldest, ritual/military) disappeared completely
  • "Arya" is NOT a race: "Arya" means "noble" or "civilised" in Sanskrit — it is a cultural-linguistic identity. Modern genetic studies identify an Aryan-related ancestry (Steppe_MLBA) but it is not a racial category. UPSC often frames questions around the Aryan debate
  • Vedic literature hierarchy: Vedas (Samhitas) → Brahmanas (ritual prose) → Aranyakas (forest texts, transitional) → Upanishads (philosophy). Plus Upavedas (applied knowledge) and Vedangas (6 auxiliary limbs). None of the Vedas were written down initially — all oral tradition (Shruti = "heard")
FeatureEarly Vedic (1500–1000 BCE)Later Vedic (1000–600 BCE)
GeographySapta Sindhu (Punjab / northwest)Ganga-Yamuna Doab → entire Ganga plains (Aryavarta)
EconomyPastoral; cattle = wealth; barterAgricultural; iron plough; PGW culture; trade guilds begin
PolityTribal (Jana); Sabha, Samiti, Vidatha; no fixed territoryJanapadas (territorial); Rajasuya, Ashvamedha; Vidatha gone
Society4 Varnas not rigid; women had rights; no Gotra rulesVarna→Jati (hereditary); women excluded from Upanayana; Gotra rules
ReligionNature worship; Indra, Agni, Varuna, Soma; no idolsElaborate yajnas; Prajapati dominant; Upanishads; karma-samsara
LiteratureRigveda onlySamaveda, Yajurveda, Atharvaveda; Brahmanas; Aranyakas; Upanishads
🔍 Examiner's Angle

UPSC frequently tests: Sabha vs Samiti vs Vidatha (which disappeared?); Vedic Literature hierarchy; Boghaz Koi inscription; Shatapatha Brahmana (eastward Aryan migration); Gayatri mantra (which Veda/metre?); 16 Sanskaras (which is the 11th = Upanayana?); Upanishad key doctrines (Tat tvam asi); Vedangas (6 limbs and their body-part analogies). Mains tests: Early vs Later Vedic society comparison; women's status; Varna to Jati transition.

📜 The Early Vedic / Rig Vedic Age (1500–1000 BCE)

1A. Original Home and Identity of the Aryans

The "Aryan Question" — where the Aryans came from — is one of the most debated topics in Indian historiography. "Arya" in Sanskrit means noble or civilised — it is a cultural and linguistic identity, not a racial one.

Major Theories on the Original Home of Aryans

Central Asian / Pontic Steppe Theory MOST ACCEPTED

Aryans originated in the Pontic-Caspian steppe (modern Ukraine/Kazakhstan). Proposed by Max Müller and others; now supported by modern genetic studies (Steppe_MLBA ancestry). Supported by: horse + chariot use; common Indo-European languages; Boghaz Koi inscription (1380 BCE, Turkey) showing Vedic gods Mitra, Varuna, Indra, Nasatya

Arctic Home Theory

Proposed by Bal Gangadhar Tilak in The Arctic Home in the Vedas (1903). Based on astronomical references in the Rigveda suggesting very long days/nights. Now not widely accepted by mainstream archaeologists

Indigenous / Out of India Theory (OIT)

Aryans were indigenous to India; Vedic culture originated in India and spread outward. Proponents: Dayananda Saraswati, some modern Indian scholars. The 2019 Rakhigarhi DNA study partly used to support this but the genetic data shows a distinct Steppe ancestry component

BMAC Connection Theory

Bactria-Margiana Archaeological Complex (c. 2200–1700 BCE, modern Turkmenistan/Afghanistan) — some scholars identify this as a staging post where Aryans absorbed local Bactrian religious elements before entering India. Viktor Sarianidi excavated BMAC sites

🔑 Boghaz Koi Inscription (Most Important External Evidence)

A 1907 discovery in Turkey (Asia Minor), dated c. 1380 BCE — a treaty between Hittite king Suppiluliuma I and Mitanni king Mattiwaza. It invokes as divine witnesses: Mitra, Varuna, Indra, Nasatya (Ashvins) — all Vedic deities. This is the most important external inscription for Aryan identity in UPSC. Also: the Kikkuli text (c. 1400 BCE, Hittite) uses Indo-Aryan horse-training terms: aika (1), tera (3), panza (5), satta (7), na (9).

1B. Geographical Background of the Early Vedic Period
  • Sapta Sindhu ("Land of Seven Rivers") = the primary geographical setting of the Rigveda. The seven rivers: Sindhu (Indus), Vitasta (Jhelum), Asikni (Chenab), Parushni (Ravi), Vipasa (Beas), Shutudri (Sutlej), and Saraswati (now dried)
  • Region = modern Punjab (Pakistan and India) and adjacent northwest; NOT yet in the Ganga plains
  • Saraswati = the most praised river in the Rigveda ("best of mothers, best of rivers, best of goddesses"); its drying up after c. 2000 BCE created a major disruption for both IVC and early Vedic societies
  • Battle of Ten Kings (Dasarajna War): Mentioned in Rigveda (Mandala 7); fought on the river Parushni (Ravi); King Sudas of the Bharatas defeated a coalition of 10 tribes (Purus, Anus, Druhyus, etc.); the Bharatas became the dominant Rigvedic tribe; gives the subcontinent its name Bharata
  • The Rigveda mentions Afghanistan (Kubha/Kabul river), showing Aryans entered from the northwest through the Hindu Kush/Khyber Pass
1C. Society of the Early Vedic Age

Social Units (Ascending Order)

UnitSanskrit TermHeadEquivalent
FamilyKulaKulapa / GrihapatiNuclear/extended family
VillageGramaGramaniVillage; basic economic unit
Clan / Sub-tribeVisVispatiCollection of villages
TribeJanaRajan / JanapatihPolitical/military unit
People / NationRashtraRajanRarely used; emerging concept

Important Functionaries of Early Vedic Society EXAM TESTED

  • Rajan (Raja) = tribal chief; not a hereditary monarch; could be elected/accepted by the tribe; primarily a war leader and protector
  • Purohita = chief priest; most important functionary after the Raja; royal counsellor; performed sacrifices; had great influence over the king; the relationship between king and purohita was sacrosanct
  • Senani = commander of the tribal army (military head)
  • Gramani = village headman; led the village group in battle; later became more of a civilian administrator
  • Vrajapati = leader of the pastoral community/grazing ground; important in the cattle-economy
  • Kulapas = heads of extended family groups
  • Spas = spies; intelligence functionaries of the tribe
  • Dutas = messengers between tribes
  • No bureaucracy, no taxation system, no standing army — all features of a tribal society

Tribal Families / Major Tribes Mentioned in Rigveda

  • Bharatas — most prominent tribe; Sudas was their famous king; won the Battle of Ten Kings; gave India its name Bharata
  • Purus — allied with Bharatas; among the important tribes of the Sapta Sindhu region
  • Anus, Druhyus, Yadus, Turvashas, Purus — the five major tribes (Pancha Jana) frequently mentioned, often in opposition to the Bharatas in the Dasarajna war coalition
  • Kurus — later became dominant in the Later Vedic period; Kurukshetra (land of the Kurus) becomes the cultural heartland
  • Panchalas — five allied clans; important in Later Vedic period; Kuru-Panchala alliance was the cultural centre
  • Tritsus — the sub-clan of King Sudas of the Bharatas
  • Matsyas, Krivis, Srinjayas — other important tribes
  • Tribal identity was based on Jana (tribe) not territory; political loyalty was to the tribe, not to a fixed land

Status of Women in Early Vedic Society

  • Women had relatively high status compared to Later Vedic and later periods
  • Gargi and Maitreyi — famous women philosophers mentioned in the Brihadaranyaka Upanishad (Gargi challenges Yajnavalkya in open debate)
  • Brahmavadinis — women who pursued lifelong Vedic study (celibate scholars)
  • Women could attend Sabha (tribal assembly)
  • No purdah system; no child marriage; widow remarriage (Niyoga) existed
  • Women participated in yajnas (sacrifices) alongside their husbands
  • Marriage age was post-puberty; women had some choice in selecting husbands (Swayamvara concept)
1D. Tribal Polity — The Three Assemblies
AssemblySanskritCompositionFunctionsLater Vedic fate
Vidatha Vidatha Both men AND women; whole tribal community; oldest assembly Ritual activities; distribution of booty after raids; military discussions; collective economic decisions; not primarily governance Disappeared completely — not found in Later Vedic texts
Sabha Sabha Council of elders; upper-class restricted membership; women (viranganas) attended in Early Vedic Judicial and administrative functions; village/local governance; could advise the Raja; dice games also held here Survived but became more aristocratic; women excluded; lost popular character
Samiti Samiti General tribal assembly; broader participation including commoners; largest of the three Could elect or depose the Raja; legislative/deliberative functions; major tribal decisions; war and peace Survived but lost power as monarchical authority grew; ultimately irrelevant by Janapada era
📌 UPSC Memory Trick for Assemblies

Vidatha = Vanished (oldest but gone by Later Vedic) | Samiti = Sovereign people (could elect/depose king) | Sabha = Selective elders council. The Rigveda mentions all three; AV (Atharvaveda) describes Sabha and Samiti as "the two daughters of Prajapati."

1E. Economy of the Early Vedic Period
  • Primarily pastoral: Cattle was the primary form of wealth; the word for war — Gavishti — literally means "search for cows." Cattle raid (Gavishti) was the most common form of conflict
  • Aghanya = "not to be killed" — applied to cows, showing the sacred status of cattle even in this period
  • Agriculture was secondary but practiced — main crop: Yava (barley); also wheat (some references)
  • Barter economy — no coins; Nishka (gold ornament) and Satamana used as units of exchange (weight-based value)
  • Crafts: Carpentry (Rathakara = chariot-maker was a high-status craft); weaving; pottery; leather work; metalwork
  • Ayas = metal mentioned in Rigveda; debated whether it refers to copper/bronze or iron; most scholars say copper/bronze in Early Vedic; "Shyama ayas" (dark metal) = iron appears in Later Vedic
  • River trade and inter-tribal exchange existed; Pani = merchant class (not well-regarded in Rigveda; associated with hoarding wealth)
  • No taxation per se; gifts to kings (Bali) were voluntary; tribute from defeated tribes
1F. Religion and Culture of the Early Vedic Period

Major Deities of the Rig Vedic Age HIGH FREQUENCY

DeityDomainHymns in RVKey Attributes
IndraThunder, rain, war~250 hymns (most)Purandara (fort-destroyer); Vritrahan (slayer of Vritra/drought demon); rides chariot; king of gods; soma-drinker
AgniFire~200 hymns (2nd most)Intermediary between humans and gods; carrier of offerings (Havyavahana); invoked at every sacrifice; present in all three worlds
VarunaCosmic order (Rita)~10 suktas dedicatedGuardian of Rita (cosmic/moral order); omniscient; punishes sin; prayers for forgiveness; associated with water and night sky; most ethical deity of the Vedic pantheon
SomaRitual plant / drinkAll of Mandala 9Deity AND ritual drink; Pavamana (purified); associated with immortality, inspiration, and the moon; Soma sacrifice was the most important Vedic ritual
SuryaSunNumerous hymnsSavitri (aspect of Surya); the Gayatri mantra (Rig 3.62.10) is addressed to Savitr; Mitra and Surya often paired
UshasDawn~20 hymnsBeautiful female deity; most personified goddess; daughter of heaven (Dyaus); drives away darkness; symbol of renewal
RudraStorms, disease, healing3 hymns in RVFierce, terrifying; archer god; both destroyer and healer; ancestor of later Shiva; Shatarudriya hymn in Yajurveda is dedicated to him
MitraLight, contracts, friendshipOften paired with VarunaGuardian of covenants and friendship; Mitra-Varuna are the two Adityas most frequently invoked together
VishnuMinor solar deity in RVOnly 5 hymnsKnown for his "three strides" (Trivikrama); not yet the major deity he becomes in later Hinduism; his importance grows enormously in the Epic-Puranic period
Ashvins (Nasatya)Twin physicians of gods~50 hymnsAssociated with healing, dawn, and rescue; horses and chariots; mentioned in Boghaz Koi inscription as divine witnesses

Key Features of Rigvedic Religion

  • Nature worship: Gods personify natural forces — Indra (storm), Agni (fire), Varuna (sky/water), Surya (sun), Ushas (dawn)
  • No idol worship, no temples in the Early Vedic period — worship through hymns (stuti) and fire sacrifices (yajna)
  • Rita (Rta) = cosmic and moral order; Varuna is its guardian; violation of Rita = sin (enas); prayers to Varuna for forgiveness
  • The Rigveda's Nasadiya Sukta (Mandala 10, Hymn 129) = the Creation Hymn — one of the world's oldest philosophical texts; questions the origin of the universe with remarkable skepticism
  • Purushasukta (Mandala 10, Hymn 90) = earliest reference to the four Varnas; describes how Brahmin came from the mouth, Kshatriya from the arms, Vaishya from the thighs, and Shudra from the feet of the cosmic Purusha; this is a late addition to the Rigveda
  • Sacrifice (Yajna) was central — Soma Yajna (most important); Ashvamedha; Rajasuya

Inscriptions of the Early Vedic / Rig Vedic Age

  • Boghaz Koi (Boghazkoy) Inscription, Turkey (c. 1380 BCE): Treaty between Hittite and Mitanni kings; invokes Mitra, Varuna, Indra, Nasatya as divine witnesses — the single most important inscription for Aryan identity and external Vedic presence. Discovered in 1907 by Hugo Winckler
  • Kikkuli Text (c. 1400 BCE, Hittite): Horse-training manual using Indo-Aryan technical terms — aika (1), tera (3), panza (5), satta (7), na (9); proves Aryan-speaking horse trainers were active in the Near East
  • Note: The Rigveda itself was an oral text (Shruti) — not inscribed. There are no inscriptions FROM within the Vedic period in India itself; the Vedic texts were memorized and transmitted orally with extraordinary precision. India's first major inscriptions are Ashoka's (3rd century BCE)
  • The Harappan/Indus Script seals (pre-Vedic, IVC) remain undeciphered — they are NOT Vedic inscriptions

📖 The Later Vedic Age (1000–600 BCE)

2A. Geographical Background of the Later Vedic Age
  • Aryans moved eastward from the Sapta Sindhu (Punjab) into the Ganga-Yamuna Doab and further into the entire Ganga plains — reaching Kosala, Videha, Panchala, Kuru territories
  • The region was now called Aryavarta (land of the Aryans) — roughly the entire northern Indian subcontinent
  • Painted Grey Ware (PGW) Culture (c. 1100–600 BCE): archaeological culture associated with Later Vedic Aryans in the upper Gangetic plains; grey pottery with black painted designs; found at Hastinapura, Kurukshetra, Mathura, Ahichhatra; indicates iron tool use and agricultural expansion
  • Shatapatha Brahmana evidence: The story of Videgha Madhava — he carries the sacred fire (Agni) eastward from the Saraswati river, crossing the Sadanira (Gandak) river into Videha (Bihar); Agni burns the forests allowing settlement; this is a mythological account of the eastward Aryan expansion
  • Kuru-Panchala region (Haryana + western UP) became the cultural heartland of Later Vedic civilisation; Hastinapura was a major centre (Mahabharata association)
  • By 600 BCE, Aryan settlements had spread across the entire Ganga plains up to Bengal
2B. Social Organization of the Later Vedic Age

Varna System — From Occupation-Based to Hereditary HIGH PRIORITY

  • In the Early Vedic period, the four Varnas (Brahmin, Kshatriya, Vaishya, Shudra) were not rigid or hereditary — they reflected occupational roles
  • In the Later Vedic period, Varna became rigidly hereditary — determined by birth, not occupation; this was the beginning of the caste system
  • Brahmin vs Kshatriya conflict: The two upper Varnas competed for supremacy; Brahmins claimed ritual authority and social precedence; Kshatriyas claimed political power; this tension is reflected in the Vishwamitra-Vasishtha conflict and later in the rise of heterodox movements (Buddhism/Jainism, which attracted Kshatriya patronage)
  • Shudras — fourth Varna — were excluded from the Upanayana ceremony and Vedic study; their status deteriorated further in this period
  • The Gotra system (patrilineal clan identity tracing descent from a Vedic rishi) became important for marriage rules — same-gotra marriages were prohibited (exogamy)
  • Women's status declined sharply: excluded from Upanayana (sacred thread); could not attend sabhas; child marriages began appearing; Manu Smriti later codified these restrictions

Four Ashramas (Stages of Life) EXAM TESTED

1. Brahmacharya (Student Stage)

Period of Vedic study; celibacy; living with the guru (Gurukula); begins with Upanayana ceremony; lasts 12–24 years; develops discipline and knowledge

2. Grihastha (Householder Stage)

Most important Ashrama; family life, work, procreation; fulfilling the three debts (Rina) — to gods (devas), ancestors (pitrs), sages (rishis); centre of social and economic life

3. Vanaprastha (Forest Dweller / Retired)

Gradual withdrawal from worldly life; retreating to the forest; continuing some rituals; supporting grown children; transition to renunciation; spouse may accompany

4. Sannyasa (Renunciation)

Complete detachment from worldly life; no fixed abode; seeking moksha (liberation); no fire, no rituals, no family ties; wandering mendicant; the ideal stage for philosophical pursuit

Note: The Ashrama system applied mainly to twice-born (dvija) males — Brahmin, Kshatriya, Vaishya. Shudras and women were largely excluded.

The 16 Sanskaras (Sacraments) UPSC ASKED

The 16 Sanskaras mark major life transitions from conception to death. They purify the individual at each stage of life:

#SanskaraMeaning / Occasion
1GarbhadhanaConception rite — performed before/after conception to ensure a healthy child
2Pumsavana3rd month of pregnancy; rite for begetting a male child
3SimantonnayanaHair-parting ceremony during pregnancy (6th/7th month); for safe delivery
4JatakarmaBirth ritual — performed before the umbilical cord is cut; father whispers sacred sounds into baby's ear
5NamakaranaNaming ceremony; 10–12 days after birth; child given a formal name
6NishkramanaFirst outing; 4th month; child first taken outside the house to see the sun
7AnnaprashanaFirst solid food ceremony; 6th month; rice or cooked grain fed for the first time
8ChudakaranaFirst haircut / tonsure; 1–3 years; scalp lock (Shikha) kept
9KarnavedhaEar piercing; 3rd–5th year; for both boys and girls
10VidyarambhaBeginning of formal education; child writes first letters in rice
11Upanayana ⭐Sacred thread ceremony (Yajnopavita) — most important Sanskara; marks entry into studenthood (Brahmacharya ashrama); performed for twice-born males at ages 8 (Brahmin), 11 (Kshatriya), 12 (Vaishya); Gayatri mantra first imparted
12VedarambhaBeginning of Vedic study with the guru; formal initiation into learning
13KeshantaFirst shaving of beard; marks transition to adulthood
14SamavartanaCompletion of studentship; farewell to the guru; return home; the student takes a ritual bath (Snataka)
15VivahaMarriage ceremony — marks entry into Grihastha Ashrama; most elaborate of all Sanskaras
16AntyeshtiFuneral rites (last rite); cremation; 10-day mourning; Shraddha (ancestor rites)
2C. Political Organization of the Later Vedic Age
  • Jana → Janapada: The tribal unit (Jana) evolved into a territorial state (Janapada = "foothold of the tribe"); political identity shifted from tribe to territory
  • Kings became more powerful; monarchy became hereditary; the earlier democratic tribal assemblies (Samiti, Sabha) declined in importance
  • Rajasuya yajna = royal consecration ceremony; established the king's divine authority; complex multi-day ritual; king rode a chariot around the kingdom symbolising sovereignty
  • Ashvamedha (Horse Sacrifice) = assertion of territorial sovereignty; a white horse was released and allowed to roam freely for one year; wherever the horse wandered became the king's claimed territory (or a battle was fought); at year's end, the horse was sacrificed in a grand ceremony; only the most powerful kings could perform it
  • Vajapeya = chariot race ritual; preceded the Rajasuya; king symbolically wins a chariot race to assert dominance
  • New political titles: Samrat (overlord of all kings), Virat (great king), Svaraj (independent king), Bhoja (sharing king), Rajan (base title)
  • Tribal Assemblies declined: Vidatha had already disappeared; Sabha became more aristocratic; Samiti lost legislative authority; real power concentrated in the king + purohita + senani
  • New functionaries (bureaucracy emerging): Bhagadugha (tax collector), Sangrahitri (treasurer), Kshattri (chamberlain), Suta (charioteer/royal herald/bard), Aksavapa (keeper of dice/gambling superintendent)
2D. Economy of the Later Vedic Age
  • Shift to agriculture: Iron tools (iron plough, iron axe) enabled clearing of the dense forests of the Ganga plains for cultivation; agriculture replaced cattle-herding as the primary economic activity
  • PGW Culture: Painted Grey Ware culture is archaeologically linked to the use of iron in the upper Ganga plains (c. 1000–600 BCE)
  • Krishna ayas / Shyama ayas = iron ("black metal") mentioned in Later Vedic texts; iron tools and weapons revolutionised both agriculture and warfare
  • Main crops: Rice (Vrihi) and Wheat (Godhuma) become more prominent alongside barley; sugarcane cultivation begins
  • Craft specialization: Potters (Kumbhakara), weavers (Tantuvaya), carpenters (Takshan), ironsmiths, goldsmiths, physicians (Bhishaj), moneylenders (Kusidin) — all mentioned
  • Trade: Merchant guilds (Sreni) begin forming; the term Vanik/Vanij (merchant) appears more frequently; trade routes along rivers develop
  • Money: Nishka (gold ornament), Satamana, Krishnala (seeds used as weight standard) — still pre-coin but more developed exchange mechanisms
  • Taxation: Bali (tribute/voluntary offering to king) becomes more systematic; Bhaga (king's share of produce = 1/6th) mentioned; professional tax collectors (Bhagadugha) appear
2E. Religion and Culture of the Later Vedic Age

Major Deities — Changed Hierarchy

  • Prajapati replaces Indra as the most important deity — creator god; lord of all creatures; abstract philosophical concept replacing the warrior nature deity
  • Rudra and Vishnu gain importance — forerunners of Shaivism and Vaishnavism
  • Indra's status declines; Agni and Soma remain important for rituals but are less spiritually central
  • Brahmanical dominance: Complex, expensive, multi-day Srauta sacrifices required professional priests; Brahmins became indispensable intermediaries
  • 4 Vedic priests for major sacrifices: Hotri (Rigveda reciter), Adhvaryu (Yajurveda; performs ritual actions), Udgátri (Samaveda chanter), Brahman (Atharvaveda; supervisor/overseer of entire ritual)
  • Philosophical revolution — Upanishads: As rituals became excessively complex and expensive, a counter-movement developed — the Upanishadic philosophy moved from ritual (karma-kanda) to inquiry (jnana-kanda); concepts of Brahman (universal soul), Atman (individual soul), Karma, Samsara, Moksha, Dharma developed
  • This Upanishadic revolution directly inspired the heterodox movements (Buddhism, Jainism) that arose at the end of this period

📚 Vedic Literature — The Complete Hierarchy

3A. The Four Vedas (Samhitas)

The Vedas are Shruti ("heard" — directly revealed to sages) as opposed to Smriti ("remembered" — humanly composed). They were transmitted orally with extreme precision for centuries before being written. Each Veda has four parts: Samhita (core text) → Brahmana → Aranyaka → Upanishad.

VedaContentHymns/VersesAssociated PriestKey Facts
Rigveda Hymns (Suktas) to deities 10 Mandalas, 1028 Suktas, ~10,552 mantras Hotri (reciter) Oldest text of the world still in use; composed c. 1500–1000 BCE; Mandalas 2–7 = oldest (family/Gotra books); Mandala 9 = Soma hymns; Mandala 10 = latest (Purushasukta, Nasadiya Sukta); Gayatri Mantra in 3.62.10
Samaveda Melodies / musical chants ~1875 verses (mostly from Rigveda, set to music) Udgátri (chanter) "Veda of melodies"; considered the source of Indian classical music; 3 recensions (Kauthuma, Ranayaniya, Jaiminiya); virtually all verses borrowed from Rigveda but with musical notation; least independent content
Yajurveda Sacrificial formulas (prose + verse) ~1875 verses + prose passages Adhvaryu (ritual performer) Two recensions: Krishna (Black) Yajurveda (Taittiriya Samhita — mixes mantras with prose explanations) and Shukla (White) Yajurveda (Vajasaneyi Samhita — mantras and prose separated); Shukla YV's Brahmana = Shatapatha Brahmana (most important of all Brahmanas)
Atharvaveda Spells, charms, folk beliefs, healing ~731 hymns + ~6000 mantras in 20 books Brahman (supervisor) Called "Veda of magic"; closest to popular religion; contains charms against disease, enemies, demons; earliest medical knowledge (precursor to Ayurveda); also has philosophical hymns (Skambha Sukta on cosmology); only partially derived from Rigveda; long not considered a Veda by orthodox tradition
3B. Brahmanas
  • What they are: Prose texts attached to each Veda explaining the meaning, origin, and correct procedure of Vedic sacrifices (karma-kanda = ritual action portion)
  • Composed c. 900–700 BCE; written in later, more developed Sanskrit
  • Each Veda has its own Brahmana(s):
VedaBrahmana(s)Key Content
RigvedaAitareya Brahmana, Kaushitaki (Sankhayana) BrahmanaAitareya contains Coronation ceremony; story of Shunahshepa
SamavedaPanchavimsha (Tandya Maha) Brahmana, Jaiminiya BrahmanaDetails of Soma sacrifices and chants
Shukla YajurvedaShatapatha Brahmana ⭐Most important Brahmana; most voluminous; contains story of Videgha Madhava (eastward Aryan migration); detailed yajna descriptions; attributed to Yajnavalkya; contains early versions of the Upanishad philosophy
Krishna YajurvedaTaittiriya BrahmanaRitual details for Krishna YV sacrifices
AtharvavedaGopatha BrahmanaLeast important; deals with Atharvan practices
3C. Aranyakas (Forest Texts)
  • Aranya = forest; these texts were composed and studied in forest retreats, meant for those in the Vanaprastha (retired/forest dweller) stage of life
  • Transitional texts between the ritualistic Brahmanas and the philosophical Upanishads; they begin interpreting sacrifice symbolically rather than literally — a dangerous/secret knowledge not meant to be shared in the village
  • Each Veda has associated Aranyakas — e.g., Aitareya Aranyaka (Rigveda), Taittiriya Aranyaka (Krishna Yajurveda), Brihadaranyaka (which transitions into the most important Upanishad)
  • Contain meditations on the inner meaning of rituals; some contain early Upanishadic passages
3D. Upanishads (Vedanta)
  • Etymology: Upa (near) + ni (down) + shad (to sit) = "sitting near the teacher" — refers to secret knowledge imparted by guru to disciple in close personal setting
  • Also called Vedanta (Veda + anta = end of the Vedas) — both the final texts of the Vedic corpus and the philosophical culmination of Vedic thought
  • Number: 108 Upanishads traditionally; 12–13 are principal (Mukhya) Upanishads recognised by Adi Shankaracharya
  • Period: c. 800–200 BCE (most principal ones c. 800–500 BCE)

Key Upanishads and Their Significance

UpanishadVedaKey Content / Claim to Fame
Brihadaranyaka ⭐Shukla YajurvedaLargest Upanishad; Yajnavalkya's dialogues (with Gargi, Maitreyi, King Janaka); theory of Brahman-Atman; Aham Brahmasmi ("I am Brahman")
Chandogya ⭐SamavedaLongest Upanishad; Tat tvam asi ("That thou art" — Atman = Brahman); Uddalaka Aruni teaches his son Shvetaketu; Satyakama Jabala story (low-birth student accepted by honesty)
KenaSamavedaBrahman as the ultimate power behind all senses and actions; "That which cannot be seen by the eye…"
KathaKrishna YajurvedaNachiketa's conversation with Yama (god of death) about the nature of the Self and death; doctrine of Atman's immortality
MundakaAtharvavedaPara (higher) vs Apara (lower) knowledge; Satyameva Jayate ("Truth alone triumphs") — India's national motto from Mundaka 3.1.6
MandukyaAtharvavedaShortest (12 verses); the four states of consciousness (waking, dreaming, deep sleep, turiya); significance of Om (AUM)
IshaShukla YajurvedaShortest verse Upanishad; God pervades all creation; unity of knowledge and action

Core Philosophical Concepts of the Upanishads

Brahman

The universal, ultimate reality underlying all existence — formless, infinite, eternal, all-pervasive. Not a personal god but the impersonal Absolute

Atman

The individual self/soul — present in every living being. The Upanishads' core teaching: Atman = Brahman (individual soul = universal soul)

Karma & Samsara

Karma = actions and their consequences binding the soul to Samsara (cycle of rebirth/transmigration). Good karma leads to higher rebirth; bad to lower

Moksha

Liberation from the Samsara cycle through jnana (knowledge of Brahman-Atman identity). Moksha = release from rebirth; the ultimate goal of Vedic philosophy

3E. Upavedas (Applied / Subsidiary Vedas)
UpavedaSubjectAssociated Veda
AyurvedaScience of life / MedicineRigveda (or Atharvaveda)
DhanurvedaScience of archery and warfareYajurveda
GandharvavedaScience of music, dance and dramaSamaveda
Sthapathyaveda / ArthashastraArchitecture, civil engineering (Vastu)Atharvaveda
3F. Vedangas — The Six Limbs of the Vedas VERY HIGH FREQUENCY

The Vedangas are six auxiliary disciplines essential for correctly understanding and performing Vedic rituals. They are Vedanga = Veda + anga (limb) — the body-parts of the Vedas:

VedangaSubjectBody Part AnalogyKey Text / Fact
ShikshaPhonetics — correct pronunciation of Vedic sounds, accent, quantity, toneNose (nasika) — "breath/sound" of the VedasPaniniya Shiksha; essential because mispronounced mantras were believed to produce opposite effects
KalpaRitual procedure — rules for sacrifices; includes Shrauta Sutras (Vedic sacrifices), Grihya Sutras (domestic rituals), Dharma Sutras (social law), Sulba Sutras (altar geometry)Hands — "action" of the VedasSulba Sutras contain early geometry — the Pythagorean theorem stated centuries before Pythagoras; Baudhayana Sulba Sutra is the oldest
VyakaranaGrammar — structure and rules of Sanskrit languageMouth (mukha) — "speech" of the VedasPanini's Ashtadhyayi (c. 4th century BCE) — 3,959 sutras; the most scientific grammar ever composed for any language; Ferdinand de Saussure called it the foundation of modern linguistics
NiruktaEtymology — explanation of difficult, archaic Vedic words and their originsEar (shrotra) — "hearing/understanding" of the VedasYaska's Nirukta (c. 7th–6th century BCE) — oldest known work on etymology in any language; without it, many Vedic words would be incomprehensible
ChhandaProsody / Metre — rhythmic structure of Vedic hymnsFeet (pada) — "movement" of the VedasMajor Vedic metres: Gayatri (3×8 = 24 syllables; most sacred), Tristubh (4×11 = 44; most common in Rigveda), Jagati (4×12 = 48), Anushtubh (4×8 = 32; used in epics)
JyotishaAstronomy / Astrology — calculating correct timing for rituals based on celestial positionsEye (chakshus) — "vision/foresight" of the VedasVedanga Jyotisha — oldest Indian astronomical text; contains rules for determining auspicious times for sacrifices; tracks sun, moon, and nakshatras (lunar mansions)
📌 Memory Device for Vedangas

Shiksha (Nose) — Kalpa (Hands) — Vyakarana (Mouth) — Nirukta (Ear) — Chhanda (Feet) — Jyotisha (Eye)
Mnemonic: "Shiv Ka Vahan Nandi Chalaye Jaldi" (Shiva's vehicle Nandi runs fast)
